<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Woman-power_emblem.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Woman-power_emblem.svg/170px-Woman-power_emblem.svg.png" width="170" height="275" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Woman-power_emblem.svg/255px-Woman-power_emblem.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Woman-power_emblem.svg/340px-Woman-power_emblem.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="648" data-file-height="1048" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Woman-power_emblem.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A generic symbol for feminism</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Feminism</b> is a range of movements and ideologies that share a common goal: to define, establish, and achieve <a href="/wiki/Feminism_and_equality" title="Feminism and equality">equal</a> political, economic, cultural, personal, and social <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_rights" title="Women's rights">rights for women</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hawkesworth_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hawkesworth-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Beasley_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beasley-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup> This includes seeking to establish equal opportunities for women in education and employment. A <b>feminist</b> advocates or supports the rights and equality of women.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Feminist movements have campaigned and continue to campaign for <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_rights" title="Women's rights">women's rights</a>, including the right to <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_suffrage" title="Women's suffrage">vote</a>, to hold public office, <a href="/wiki/Right_to_work" title="Right to work">to work</a>, to earn fair wages or <a href="/wiki/Equal_pay_for_equal_work" title="Equal pay for equal work">equal pay</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Right_to_property" title="Right to property">own property</a>, <a href="/wiki/Right_to_education" title="Right to education">to education</a>, to enter contracts, to have equal rights within marriage, and to have <a href="/wiki/Maternity_leave" title="Maternity leave" class="mw-redirect">maternity leave</a>. Feminists have also worked to promote bodily <a href="/wiki/Autonomy" title="Autonomy">autonomy</a> and integrity, and to protect women and girls from rape, <a href="/wiki/Sexual_harassment" title="Sexual harassment">sexual harassment</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Domestic_violence" title="Domestic violence">domestic violence</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Echols_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Echols-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Feminist campaigns are generally considered to be one of the main forces behind major historical societal changes for women's rights, particularly in the West, where they are near-universally credited with having achieved <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_suffrage" title="Women's suffrage">women's suffrage</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gender_neutrality_in_English" title="Gender neutrality in English">gender neutrality in English</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reproductive_rights" title="Reproductive rights">reproductive rights</a> for women (including access to <a href="/wiki/Contraceptive" title="Contraceptive" class="mw-redirect">contraceptives</a> and <a href="/wiki/Support_for_the_legalization_of_abortion" title="Support for the legalization of abortion" class="mw-redirect">abortion</a>), and the right to enter into contracts and <a href="/wiki/Property" title="Property">own property</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Messer-Davidow_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Messer-Davidow-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> Although feminist advocacy is and has been mainly focused on women's rights, some feminists, including <a href="/wiki/Bell_hooks" title="Bell hooks">bell hooks</a>, argue for the inclusion of <a href="/wiki/Men%27s_liberation" title="Men's liberation" class="mw-redirect">men's liberation</a> within its aims because men are also harmed by traditional <a href="/wiki/Gender_roles" title="Gender roles" class="mw-redirect">gender roles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-hooks_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hooks-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Feminist_theory" title="Feminist theory">Feminist theory</a>, which emerged from <a href="/wiki/Feminist_movement" title="Feminist movement">feminist movements</a>, aims to understand the nature of gender inequality by examining women's social roles and lived experience; it has developed theories in a variety of disciplines in order to respond to issues such as the social construction of gender.<sup id="cite_ref-Chodorow1989_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chodorow1989-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-gilligan1977_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gilligan1977-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Some forms of feminism have been <a href="/wiki/Feminism#Civil_rights_movement_and_anti-racism" title="Feminism">criticized</a> for taking into account only white, middle-class, and educated perspectives. This led to the creation of ethnically specific or multiculturalist forms of feminism.<sup id="cite_ref-Weedon_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weedon-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Feminist_Suffrage_Parade_in_New_York_City,_1912.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Feminist_Suffrage_Parade_in_New_York_City%2C_1912.jpeg/220px-Feminist_Suffrage_Parade_in_New_York_City%2C_1912.jpeg" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Feminist_Suffrage_Parade_in_New_York_City%2C_1912.jpeg/330px-Feminist_Suffrage_Parade_in_New_York_City%2C_1912.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Feminist_Suffrage_Parade_in_New_York_City%2C_1912.jpeg/440px-Feminist_Suffrage_Parade_in_New_York_City%2C_1912.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="564" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Feminist_Suffrage_Parade_in_New_York_City,_1912.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Feminist Suffrage Parade in New York City, 6 May 1912</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_feminism" title="History of feminism">History of feminism</a></div>
<div class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Protofeminism" title="Protofeminism">Protofeminism</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Charles_Fourier" title="Charles Fourier">Charles Fourier</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Utopian_socialism" title="Utopian socialism">Utopian Socialist</a> and French philosopher, is credited with having coined the word "féminisme" in 1837.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> The words "féminisme" ("feminisme") and "féminist" ("feminist") first appeared in France and the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> in 1872,<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> Great Britain in the 1890s, and the United States in 1910,<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-cott_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cott-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary">Oxford English Dictionary</a></i> lists 1852 as the year of the first appearance of "feminist"<sup id="cite_ref-oed-nist_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oed-nist-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> and 1895 for "feminism".<sup id="cite_ref-oed_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oed-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> Depending on historical moment, culture and country, feminists around the world have had different causes and goals. Most western feminist historians assert that all movements that work to obtain <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_rights" title="Women's rights">women's rights</a> should be considered feminist movements, even when they did not (or do not) apply the term to themselves.<sup id="cite_ref-spender_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-spender-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-walters_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-walters-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-kinnaird_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kinnaird-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-taylor_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taylor-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> Other historians assert that the term should be limited to the modern feminist movement and its descendants. Those historians use the label "<a href="/wiki/Protofeminist" title="Protofeminist" class="mw-redirect">protofeminist</a>" to describe earlier movements.<sup id="cite_ref-botting-houser_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-botting-houser-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The history of the modern western feminist movements is divided into three "waves".<sup id="cite_ref-Humm_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Humm-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Walker1992_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walker1992-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup> Each wave dealt with different aspects of the same feminist issues. The <a href="/wiki/First-wave_feminism" title="First-wave feminism">first wave</a> comprised <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_suffrage" title="Women's suffrage">women's suffrage</a> movements of the nineteenth and early twentieth centuries, promoting women's right to vote. The <a href="/wiki/Second-wave_feminism" title="Second-wave feminism">second wave</a> was associated with the ideas and actions of the <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_liberation_movement" title="Women's liberation movement">women's liberation movement</a> beginning in the 1960s. The second wave campaigned for legal and social equality for women. The <a href="/wiki/Third-wave_feminism" title="Third-wave feminism">third wave</a> is a continuation of, and a reaction to, the perceived failures of second-wave feminism, beginning in the 1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-Suffragettes_to_Grrls_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Suffragettes_to_Grrls-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nineteenth_and_early_twentieth_centuries">Nineteenth and early twentieth centuries</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/First-wave_feminism" title="First-wave feminism">First-wave feminism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Emmeline_Pankhurst_adresses_crowd.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Emmeline_Pankhurst_adresses_crowd.jpg/220px-Emmeline_Pankhurst_adresses_crowd.jpg" width="220" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Emmeline_Pankhurst_adresses_crowd.jpg/330px-Emmeline_Pankhurst_adresses_crowd.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Emmeline_Pankhurst_adresses_crowd.jpg/440px-Emmeline_Pankhurst_adresses_crowd.jpg 2x" data-file-width="518" data-file-height="390" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Emmeline_Pankhurst_adresses_crowd.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
After selling her home, <a href="/wiki/Emmeline_Pankhurst" title="Emmeline Pankhurst">Emmeline Pankhurst</a>, pictured in New York City in 1913, travelled constantly, giving speeches throughout Britain and the United States.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wilhelmina_Drucker_IMG0020.tif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Wilhelmina_Drucker_IMG0020.tif/lossless-page1-220px-Wilhelmina_Drucker_IMG0020.tif.png" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Wilhelmina_Drucker_IMG0020.tif/lossless-page1-330px-Wilhelmina_Drucker_IMG0020.tif.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Wilhelmina_Drucker_IMG0020.tif/lossless-page1-440px-Wilhelmina_Drucker_IMG0020.tif.png 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="3072" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wilhelmina_Drucker_IMG0020.tif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
In the Netherlands, <a href="/wiki/Wilhelmina_Drucker" title="Wilhelmina Drucker">Wilhelmina Drucker</a> (1847-1925) fought successfully for the vote and equal rights for women through political and feminist organisations she founded.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Louise_Weiss.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Louise_Weiss.jpg/220px-Louise_Weiss.jpg" width="220" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Louise_Weiss.jpg/330px-Louise_Weiss.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Louise_Weiss.jpg/440px-Louise_Weiss.jpg 2x" data-file-width="550" data-file-height="400" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Louise_Weiss.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Louise_Weiss" title="Louise Weiss">Louise Weiss</a> along with other Parisian <a href="/wiki/Suffragette" title="Suffragette">suffragettes</a> in 1935. The newspaper headline reads "The Frenchwoman Must Vote."</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/First-wave_feminism" title="First-wave feminism">First-wave feminism</a> was a period of activity during the nineteenth century and early twentieth century. In the UK and US, it focused on the promotion of equal contract, marriage, parenting, and property rights for women. By the end of the nineteenth century, activism focused primarily on gaining political power, particularly the right of women's <a href="/wiki/Suffrage" title="Suffrage">suffrage</a>, though some feminists were active in campaigning for women's sexual, <a href="/wiki/Reproductive_rights" title="Reproductive rights">reproductive</a>, and economic rights as well.<sup id="cite_ref-Freedman_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Freedman-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Women%27s_suffrage" title="Women's suffrage">Women's suffrage</a> began in Britain's <a href="/wiki/Australasia" title="Australasia">Australasian</a> colonies at the close of the 19th century, with the self-governing colonies of <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a> granting women the <a href="/wiki/Right_to_vote" title="Right to vote" class="mw-redirect">right to vote</a> in 1893 and South Australia granting female suffrage (the right to vote and stand for parliamentary office) in 1895. This was followed by Australia granting female suffrage in 1902.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">Britain</a> the <a href="/wiki/Suffragette" title="Suffragette">Suffragettes</a> and the <a href="/wiki/National_Union_of_Women%27s_Suffrage_Societies" title="National Union of Women's Suffrage Societies">Suffragists</a> campaigned for the women's vote, and in 1918 the <a href="/wiki/Representation_of_the_People_Act_1918" title="Representation of the People Act 1918">Representation of the People Act</a> was passed granting the vote to women over the age of 30 who owned houses. In 1928 this was extended to all women over twenty-one.<sup id="cite_ref-Phillips_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips-29"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Emmeline_Pankhurst" title="Emmeline Pankhurst">Emmeline Pankhurst</a> was the most notable activist, with <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> naming her one of the <a href="/wiki/Time_100:_The_Most_Important_People_of_the_Century" title="Time 100: The Most Important People of the Century">100 Most Important People of the 20th Century</a> stating: "she shaped an idea of women for our time; she shook society into a new pattern from which there could be no going back."<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> In the U.S., notable leaders of this movement included <a href="/wiki/Lucretia_Mott" title="Lucretia Mott">Lucretia Mott</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Cady_Stanton" title="Elizabeth Cady Stanton">Elizabeth Cady Stanton</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Susan_B._Anthony" title="Susan B. Anthony">Susan B. Anthony</a>, who each campaigned for the <a href="/wiki/Abolitionism_in_the_United_States" title="Abolitionism in the United States">abolition of slavery</a> prior to championing women's right to vote. These women were influenced by the <a href="/wiki/Quaker" title="Quaker" class="mw-redirect">Quaker</a> theology of spiritual equality, which asserts that men and women are equal under God.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> In the United States, first-wave feminism is considered to have ended with the passage of the <a href="/wiki/Nineteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Nineteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Nineteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution</a> (1919), granting women the right to vote in all states. The term <i>first wave</i> was coined retroactively to categorize these western movements after the term <i><a href="/wiki/Second-wave_feminism" title="Second-wave feminism">second-wave feminism</a></i> began to be used to describe a newer feminist movement that focused as much on fighting social and cultural inequalities as political inequalities.<sup id="cite_ref-Freedman_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Freedman-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DuBois_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DuBois-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Flexner_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flexner-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wheeler_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wheeler-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Stevens_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stevens-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>During the late <a href="/wiki/Qing_Dynasty" title="Qing Dynasty" class="mw-redirect">Qing period</a> and reform movements such as the <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Days%27_Reform" title="Hundred Days' Reform">Hundred Days' Reform</a>, Chinese feminists called for women's liberation from traditional roles and <a href="/wiki/Neo-Confucian" title="Neo-Confucian" class="mw-redirect">Neo-Confucian</a> gender segregation.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> Later, the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Communist_Party" title="Chinese Communist Party" class="mw-redirect">Chinese Communist Party</a> created projects aimed at integrating women into the workforce, and claimed that the revolution had successfully achieved women's liberation.<sup id="cite_ref-Dooling_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dooling-39"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>According to Nawar al-Hassan Golley, Arab feminism was closely connected with <a href="/wiki/Arab_nationalism" title="Arab nationalism">Arab nationalism</a>. In 1899, <a href="/wiki/Qasim_Amin" title="Qasim Amin">Qasim Amin</a>, considered the "father" of Arab feminism, wrote <i>The Liberation of Women</i>, which argued for legal and social reforms for women.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> He drew links between women's position in Egyptian society and nationalism, leading to the development of Cairo University and the National Movement.<sup id="cite_ref-Golley_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Golley-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1923 <a href="/wiki/Hoda_Shaarawi" title="Hoda Shaarawi" class="mw-redirect">Hoda Shaarawi</a> founded the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Feminist_Union" title="Egyptian Feminist Union">Egyptian Feminist Union</a>, became its president and a symbol of the Arab women's rights movement.<sup id="cite_ref-Golley_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Golley-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Iranian_Constitutional_Revolution" title="Iranian Constitutional Revolution" class="mw-redirect">Iranian Constitutional Revolution</a> in 1905 triggered the <a href="/wiki/Iranian_women%27s_movement" title="Iranian women's movement" class="mw-redirect">Iranian women's movement</a>, which aimed to achieve women's equality in education, marriage, careers, and legal rights.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> However, during the <a href="/wiki/Iranian_revolution" title="Iranian revolution" class="mw-redirect">Iranian revolution</a> of 1979, many of the rights that women had gained from the women's movement were systematically abolished, such as the <a href="/wiki/Iran%27s_Family_Protection_Law" title="Iran's Family Protection Law">Family Protection Law</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In France, women obtained the <a href="/wiki/Right_to_vote" title="Right to vote" class="mw-redirect">right to vote</a> only with the <a href="/wiki/Provisional_Government_of_the_French_Republic" title="Provisional Government of the French Republic">Provisional Government of the French Republic</a> of 21 April 1944. The Consultative Assembly of Algiers of 1944 proposed on 24 March 1944 to grant eligibility to women but following an amendment by <a href="/wiki/Fernand_Grenier" title="Fernand Grenier">Fernand Grenier</a>, they were given full citizenship, including the right to vote. Grenier's proposition was adopted 51 to 16. In May 1947, following the <a href="/wiki/French_legislative_election,_November_1946" title="French legislative election, November 1946">November 1946 elections</a>, the sociologist Robert Verdier minimized the "<a href="/wiki/Gender_differences" title="Gender differences" class="mw-redirect">gender gap</a>," stating in <i><a href="/wiki/Le_Populaire" title="Le Populaire">Le Populaire</a></i> that women had not voted in a consistent way, dividing themselves, as men, according to social classes. During the <a href="/wiki/Post-World_War_II_baby_boom" title="Post-World War II baby boom" class="mw-redirect">baby boom</a> period, feminism waned in importance. Wars (both World War I and World War II) had seen the provisional emancipation of some, individual, women, but post-war periods signaled the return to conservative roles.<sup id="cite_ref-Bard_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bard-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mid-twentieth_century">Mid-twentieth century</span></h3>
<p>By the mid 20th century, in some European countries, women still lacked some significant rights. Feminists in these countries continued to fight for voting rights. In <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>, women gained the right to vote in federal elections in 1971;<sup id="cite_ref-switzerland-chronology_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-switzerland-chronology-45"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup> but in the canton of <a href="/wiki/Appenzell_Innerrhoden" title="Appenzell Innerrhoden">Appenzell Innerrhoden</a> women obtained the right to vote on local issues only in 1991, when the canton was forced to do so by the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Supreme_Court_of_Switzerland" title="Federal Supreme Court of Switzerland">Federal Supreme Court of Switzerland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Liechtenstein" title="Liechtenstein">Liechtenstein</a>, women were given the right to vote by the <a href="/wiki/Liechtenstein_women%27s_suffrage_referendum,_1984" title="Liechtenstein women's suffrage referendum, 1984">women's suffrage referendum of 1984</a>. Three prior referendums held in <a href="/wiki/Liechtenstein_women%27s_suffrage_referendum,_1968" title="Liechtenstein women's suffrage referendum, 1968" class="mw-redirect">1968</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liechtenstein_women%27s_suffrage_referendum,_1971" title="Liechtenstein women's suffrage referendum, 1971">1971</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liechtenstein_women%27s_suffrage_referendum,_1973" title="Liechtenstein women's suffrage referendum, 1973" class="mw-redirect">1973</a> had failed to secure women's right to vote.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Photograph_of_American_Women_Replacing_Men_Fighting_in_Europe_-_NARA_-_535769.tif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Photograph_of_American_Women_Replacing_Men_Fighting_in_Europe_-_NARA_-_535769.tif/lossy-page1-220px-Photograph_of_American_Women_Replacing_Men_Fighting_in_Europe_-_NARA_-_535769.tif.jpg" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Photograph_of_American_Women_Replacing_Men_Fighting_in_Europe_-_NARA_-_535769.tif/lossy-page1-330px-Photograph_of_American_Women_Replacing_Men_Fighting_in_Europe_-_NARA_-_535769.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Photograph_of_American_Women_Replacing_Men_Fighting_in_Europe_-_NARA_-_535769.tif/lossy-page1-440px-Photograph_of_American_Women_Replacing_Men_Fighting_in_Europe_-_NARA_-_535769.tif.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2119" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Photograph_of_American_Women_Replacing_Men_Fighting_in_Europe_-_NARA_-_535769.tif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Photograph of American women replacing men fighting in Europe, 1945</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Feminists continued to campaign for the reform of <a href="/wiki/Family_law" title="Family law">family laws</a> which gave husbands control over their wives. Although by the 20th century <a href="/wiki/Coverture" title="Coverture">coverture</a> had been abolished in the UK and the US, in many <a href="/wiki/Continental_European" title="Continental European" class="mw-redirect">continental European</a> countries married women still had very few rights. For instance, in France married women did not receive the right to work without their husband's permission until 1965.<sup id="cite_ref-Guillaumin_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guillaumin-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Meltzer_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meltzer-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> Feminists have also worked to abolish the <a href="/wiki/Marital_rape" title="Marital rape">"marital exemption" in rape laws</a> which precluded the prosecution of husbands for the rape of their wives.<sup id="cite_ref-Allison_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Allison-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup> Earlier efforts by first-wave feminists such as <a href="/wiki/Voltairine_de_Cleyre" title="Voltairine de Cleyre">Voltairine de Cleyre</a>, <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Woodhull" title="Victoria Woodhull">Victoria Woodhull</a> and <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Clarke_Wolstenholme_Elmy" title="Elizabeth Clarke Wolstenholme Elmy">Elizabeth Clarke Wolstenholme Elmy</a> to criminalize marital rape in the late 19th century had failed;<sup id="cite_ref-Bland_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bland-50"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup> this was only achieved a century later in most Western countries, but is still not achieved in many other parts of the world.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>French philosopher <a href="/wiki/Simone_de_Beauvoir" title="Simone de Beauvoir">Simone de Beauvoir</a> provided a <a href="/wiki/Marxist" title="Marxist" class="mw-redirect">Marxist</a> solution and an <a href="/wiki/Existentialist" title="Existentialist" class="mw-redirect">existentialist</a> view on many of the questions of feminism with the publication of <i>Le Deuxième Sexe</i> (<i><a href="/wiki/The_Second_Sex" title="The Second Sex">The Second Sex</a></i>) in 1949.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> The book expressed feminists' sense of injustice. <a href="/wiki/Second-wave_feminism" title="Second-wave feminism">Second-wave feminism</a> is a feminist movement beginning in the early 1960s<sup id="cite_ref-Whelehan_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whelehan-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> and continuing to the present; as such, it coexists with <a href="/wiki/Third-wave_feminism" title="Third-wave feminism">third-wave feminism</a>. Second-wave feminism is largely concerned with issues of equality other than suffrage, such as ending discrimination.<sup id="cite_ref-Freedman_26-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Freedman-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Second-wave feminists see women's cultural and political inequalities as inextricably linked and encourage women to understand aspects of their personal lives as deeply politicized and as reflecting <a href="/wiki/Sexism" title="Sexism">sexist</a> power structures. The feminist activist and author <a href="/wiki/Carol_Hanisch" title="Carol Hanisch">Carol Hanisch</a> coined the slogan "The Personal is Political", which became synonymous with the second wave.<sup id="cite_ref-Echols_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Echols-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hanisch_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hanisch-55"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Second- and third-wave feminism in China has been characterized by a reexamination of women's roles during the communist revolution and other reform movements, and new discussions about whether women's equality has actually been fully achieved.<sup id="cite_ref-Dooling_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dooling-39"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1956, President <a href="/wiki/Gamal_Abdel_Nasser" title="Gamal Abdel Nasser">Gamal Abdel Nasser</a> of Egypt initiated "<a href="/wiki/State_feminism" title="State feminism">state feminism</a>", which outlawed discrimination based on gender and granted women's suffrage, but also blocked political activism by feminist leaders.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup> During <a href="/wiki/Anwar_Sadat" title="Anwar Sadat">Sadat</a>'s presidency, his wife, <a href="/wiki/Jehan_Sadat" title="Jehan Sadat">Jehan Sadat</a>, publicly advocated further women's rights, though Egyptian policy and society began to move away from women's equality with the new <a href="/wiki/Islamist" title="Islamist" class="mw-redirect">Islamist</a> movement and growing conservatism.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> However, some activists proposed a new feminist movement, <a href="/wiki/Islamic_feminism" title="Islamic feminism">Islamic feminism</a>, which argues for women's equality within an Islamic framework.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In Latin America, revolutions brought changes in women's status in countries such as <a href="/wiki/Role_of_women_in_Nicaraguan_Revolution" title="Role of women in Nicaraguan Revolution">Nicaragua</a>, where <a href="/wiki/Feminist_ideology_during_the_Sandinista_Revolution" title="Feminist ideology during the Sandinista Revolution" class="mw-redirect">feminist ideology during the Sandinista Revolution</a> aided women's quality of life but fell short of achieving a social and ideological change.<sup id="cite_ref-parpart_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-parpart-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_twentieth_and_early_twenty-first_centuries">Late twentieth and early twenty-first centuries</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Third-wave_feminism">Third-wave feminism</span></h4>
<p>In the early 1990s in the USA, <a href="/wiki/Third-wave_feminism" title="Third-wave feminism">third-wave feminism</a> began as a response to perceived failures of the second wave and to the backlash against initiatives and movements created by the second wave. Third-wave feminism distinguished itself from the second wave around issues of <a href="/wiki/Sexuality" title="Sexuality" class="mw-redirect">sexuality</a>, challenging female <a href="/wiki/Heterosexuality" title="Heterosexuality">heterosexuality</a> and celebrating sexuality as a means of female empowerment.<sup id="cite_ref-CultStrat_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CultStrat-60"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup> Third-wave feminism also seeks to challenge or avoid what it deems the second wave's <a href="/wiki/Essentialism" title="Essentialism">essentialist</a> definitions of <a href="/wiki/Femininity" title="Femininity">femininity</a>, which, they argue, over-emphasize the experiences of upper middle-class white women. Third-wave feminists often focus on "micro-politics" and challenge the second wave's paradigm as to what is, or is not, good for women, and tend to use a <a href="/wiki/Post-structuralism" title="Post-structuralism">post-structuralist</a> interpretation of gender and sexuality.<sup id="cite_ref-Freedman_26-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Freedman-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Henry_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Henry-61"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gillis_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gillis-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Faludi_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Faludi-63"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup> Feminist leaders rooted in the second wave, such as <a href="/wiki/Gloria_Anzald%C3%BAa" title="Gloria Anzaldúa" class="mw-redirect">Gloria Anzaldúa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bell_hooks" title="Bell hooks">bell hooks</a>, Chela Sandoval, <a href="/wiki/Cherr%C3%ADe_Moraga" title="Cherríe Moraga">Cherríe Moraga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Audre_Lorde" title="Audre Lorde">Audre Lorde</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maxine_Hong_Kingston" title="Maxine Hong Kingston">Maxine Hong Kingston</a>, and many other non-white feminists, sought to negotiate a space within feminist thought for consideration of race-related subjectivities.<sup id="cite_ref-Gillis_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gillis-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Walker_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walker-64"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Heywood_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Heywood-65"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup> Third-wave feminism also contains internal debates between <a href="/wiki/Difference_feminism" title="Difference feminism">difference feminists</a>, who believe that there are important differences between the sexes, and those who believe that there are no inherent differences between the sexes and contend that gender roles are due to <a href="/wiki/Social_conditioning" title="Social conditioning">social conditioning</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Gilligan_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gilligan-66"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Standpoint_feminism">Standpoint feminism</span></h4>
<p>Since the 1980s, <a href="/wiki/Standpoint_feminism" title="Standpoint feminism">standpoint feminists</a> have argued that the feminist movement should address global issues (such as rape, <a href="/wiki/Incest" title="Incest">incest</a>, and prostitution) and culturally specific issues (such as <a href="/wiki/Female_genital_mutilation" title="Female genital mutilation">female genital mutilation</a> in some parts of Africa and the Middle East, as well as <a href="/wiki/Glass_ceiling" title="Glass ceiling">glass ceiling</a> practices that impede women's advancement in developed economies) in order to understand how gender inequality interacts with racism, <a href="/wiki/Homophobia" title="Homophobia">homophobia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Classism" title="Classism" class="mw-redirect">classism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Colonization" title="Colonization">colonization</a> in a "matrix of domination."<sup id="cite_ref-BFT_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BFT-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Harding2003_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harding2003-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-feminism">Post-feminism</span></h4>
<p>The term <a href="/wiki/Post-feminism" title="Post-feminism" class="mw-redirect">post-feminism</a> is used to describe a range of viewpoints reacting to feminism since the 1980s. While not being "anti-feminist", post-feminists believe that women have achieved second wave goals while being critical of third wave feminist goals. The term was first used to describe a backlash against <a href="/wiki/Second-wave_feminism" title="Second-wave feminism">second-wave feminism</a>, but it is now a label for a wide range of theories that take critical approaches to previous feminist discourses and includes challenges to the second wave's ideas.<sup id="cite_ref-Wright2000_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wright2000-69"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup> Other post-feminists say that feminism is no longer relevant to today's society.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> Amelia Jones has written that the post-feminist texts which emerged in the 1980s and 1990s portrayed second-wave feminism as a monolithic entity.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup> Dorothy Chunn notes a "blaming narrative" under the post-feminist moniker, where feminists are undermined for continuing to make demands for gender equality in a "post-feminist" society, where "gender equality has (already) been achieved." According to Chunn, "many feminists have voiced disquiet about the ways in which rights and equality discourses are now used against them."<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Theory">Theory</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Feminist_theory" title="Feminist theory">Feminist theory</a></div>
<div class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Gynocriticism" title="Gynocriticism">Gynocriticism</a> and <a href="/wiki/%C3%89criture_f%C3%A9minine" title="Écriture féminine">écriture féminine</a></div>
<p>Feminist theory is the extension of feminism into theoretical or philosophical fields. It encompasses work in a variety of disciplines, including <a href="/wiki/Feminist_anthropology" title="Feminist anthropology">anthropology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Feminist_sociology" title="Feminist sociology">sociology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Feminist_economics" title="Feminist economics">economics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_studies" title="Women's studies">women's studies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Feminist_literary_criticism" title="Feminist literary criticism">literary criticism</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Zajko_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zajko-73"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Howe_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Howe-74"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Art_history#Psychoanalytic_art_history" title="Art history">art history</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Feminist_theory#Psychoanalysis" title="Feminist theory">psychoanalysis</a><sup id="cite_ref-matrixial_borderspace_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-matrixial_borderspace-76"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Feminist_philosophy" title="Feminist philosophy">philosophy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Florence_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Florence-78"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup> Feminist theory aims to understand <a href="/wiki/Gender_inequality" title="Gender inequality">gender inequality</a> and focuses on gender politics, power relations, and sexuality. While providing a critique of these social and political relations, much of feminist theory also focuses on the promotion of women's rights and interests. Themes explored in feminist theory include discrimination, <a href="/wiki/Stereotyping" title="Stereotyping" class="mw-redirect">stereotyping</a>, <a href="/wiki/Objectification" title="Objectification">objectification</a> (especially <a href="/wiki/Sexual_objectification" title="Sexual objectification">sexual objectification</a>), <a href="/wiki/Oppression" title="Oppression">oppression</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Patriarchy" title="Patriarchy">patriarchy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Chodorow1989_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chodorow1989-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-gilligan1977_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gilligan1977-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In the field of <a href="/wiki/Literary_criticism" title="Literary criticism">literary criticism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elaine_Showalter" title="Elaine Showalter">Elaine Showalter</a> describes the development of feminist theory as having three phases. The first she calls "feminist critique", in which the feminist reader examines the ideologies behind literary phenomena. The second Showalter calls "<a href="/wiki/Gynocriticism" title="Gynocriticism">gynocriticism</a>", in which the "woman is producer of textual meaning". The last phase she calls "gender theory", in which the "ideological inscription and the literary effects of the sex/gender system are explored".<sup id="cite_ref-showalter1979_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-showalter1979-79"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>This was paralleled in the 1970s by <a href="/wiki/French_structuralist_feminism" title="French structuralist feminism" class="mw-redirect">French feminists</a>, who developed the concept of <i><a href="/wiki/%C3%89criture_f%C3%A9minine" title="Écriture féminine">écriture féminine</a></i> (which translates as 'female or feminine writing').<sup id="cite_ref-Wright2000_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wright2000-69"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Helene_Cixous" title="Helene Cixous" class="mw-redirect">Helene Cixous</a> argues that writing and philosophy are <i><a href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/phallocentric" class="extiw" title="wikt:phallocentric">phallocentric</a></i> and along with other French feminists such as <a href="/wiki/Luce_Irigaray" title="Luce Irigaray">Luce Irigaray</a> emphasize "writing from the body" as a subversive exercise.<sup id="cite_ref-Wright2000_69-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wright2000-69"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup> The work of <a href="/wiki/Julia_Kristeva" title="Julia Kristeva">Julia Kristeva</a>, a feminist psychoanalyst and philosopher, and <a href="/wiki/Bracha_Ettinger" title="Bracha Ettinger" class="mw-redirect">Bracha Ettinger</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> artist and psychoanalyst, has influenced feminist theory in general and <a href="/wiki/Feminist_literary_criticism" title="Feminist literary criticism">feminist literary criticism</a> in particular. However, as the scholar Elizabeth Wright points out, "none of these French feminists align themselves with the feminist movement as it appeared in the <a href="/wiki/English-speaking_world" title="English-speaking world">Anglophone</a> world".<sup id="cite_ref-Wright2000_69-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wright2000-69"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Moi1986_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moi1986-81"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup> More recent feminist theory, such as that of <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://papers.ssrn.com/sol3/papers.cfm?abstract_id=2439294">Lisa Lucile Owens</a>, has concentrated on characterizing feminism as a universal emancipatory movement.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Movements_and_ideologies">Movements and ideologies</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Feminist_movements_and_ideologies" title="Feminist movements and ideologies">Feminist movements and ideologies</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Woman-power_emblem.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Woman-power_emblem.svg/100px-Woman-power_emblem.svg.png" width="100" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Woman-power_emblem.svg/150px-Woman-power_emblem.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Woman-power_emblem.svg/200px-Woman-power_emblem.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="648" data-file-height="1048" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Woman-power_emblem.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Symbol of feminism based on <a href="/wiki/Venus_symbol" title="Venus symbol">Venus symbol</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Many overlapping feminist movements and ideologies have developed over the years.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_movements">Political movements</span></h3>
<p>Some branches of feminism closely track the political leanings of the larger society, such as liberalism and conservatism, or focus on the environment. <a href="/wiki/Liberal_feminism" title="Liberal feminism">Liberal feminism</a> seeks individualistic equality of men and women through political and legal reform without altering the structure of society. <a href="/wiki/Radical_feminism" title="Radical feminism">Radical feminism</a> considers the male-controlled capitalist hierarchy as the defining feature of women's oppression and the total uprooting and reconstruction of society as necessary.<sup id="cite_ref-Echols_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Echols-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/List_of_conservative_feminisms" title="List of conservative feminisms">Conservative feminism</a> is conservative relative to the society in which it resides. <a href="/wiki/Individualist_feminism" title="Individualist feminism">Libertarian feminism</a> conceives of people as self-owners and therefore as entitled to freedom from coercive interference.<sup id="cite_ref-Stanford_Encyclopedia_of_Philosophy_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stanford_Encyclopedia_of_Philosophy-82"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Separatist_feminism" title="Separatist feminism">Separatist feminism</a> does not support heterosexual relationships. Lesbian feminism is thus closely related. Other feminists criticize separatist feminism as sexist.<sup id="cite_ref-hooks_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hooks-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eco-feminist" title="Eco-feminist" class="mw-redirect">Ecofeminists</a> see men's control of land as responsible for the oppression of women and destruction of the <a href="/wiki/Ecology" title="Ecology">natural environment</a>; ecofeminism has been criticised for focusing too much on a mystical connection between women and nature.<sup id="cite_ref-Biehl_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Biehl-83"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Materialist_ideologies">Materialist ideologies</span></h3>
<p>Rosemary Hennessy and Chrys Ingraham say that materialist forms of feminism grew out of Western Marxist thought and have inspired a number of different (but overlapping) movements, all of which are involved in a critique of capitalism and are focussed on ideology's relationship to women.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Marxist_feminism" title="Marxist feminism">Marxist feminism</a> argues that capitalism is the root cause of women's oppression, and that discrimination against women in domestic life and employment is an effect of capitalist ideologies.<sup id="cite_ref-Bottomore_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bottomore-85"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Socialist_feminism" title="Socialist feminism">Socialist feminism</a> distinguishes itself from Marxist feminism by arguing that women's liberation can only be achieved by working to end both the economic and cultural sources of women's oppression.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Anarcha-feminism" title="Anarcha-feminism">Anarcha-feminists</a> believe that <a href="/wiki/Class_struggle" title="Class struggle" class="mw-redirect">class struggle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anarchism" title="Anarchism">anarchy</a> against the <a href="/wiki/State_(polity)" title="State (polity)">state</a><sup id="cite_ref-farrow_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-farrow-87"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup> require struggling against <a href="/wiki/Patriarchy" title="Patriarchy">patriarchy</a>, which comes from involuntary hierarchy.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Black_and_postcolonial_ideologies">Black and postcolonial ideologies</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sara_Ahmed" title="Sara Ahmed">Sara Ahmed</a> argues that <a href="/wiki/Black_feminism" title="Black feminism">Black</a> and <a href="/wiki/Postcolonial_feminism" title="Postcolonial feminism">Postcolonial</a> feminisms pose a challenge "to some of the organizing premises of Western feminist thought."<sup id="cite_ref-Ahmed_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ahmed-88"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup> During much of its <a href="/wiki/History_of_feminism" title="History of feminism">history</a>, feminist movements and <a href="/wiki/Feminism#Theoretical_schools" title="Feminism">theoretical developments</a> were led predominantly by middle-class white women from Western Europe and North America.<sup id="cite_ref-Walker_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walker-64"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BFT_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BFT-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Narayan_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Narayan-89"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup> However women of other races have proposed alternative feminisms.<sup id="cite_ref-BFT_67-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BFT-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> This trend accelerated in the 1960s with the civil rights movement in the United States and the collapse of European colonialism in Africa, the Caribbean, parts of Latin America, and Southeast Asia. Since that time, women in <a href="/wiki/Third_World" title="Third World">developing nations</a> and <a href="/wiki/Postcolonialism" title="Postcolonialism">former colonies</a> and who are of colour or various ethnicities or living in poverty have proposed additional feminisms.<sup id="cite_ref-Narayan_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Narayan-89"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Womanism" title="Womanism">Womanism</a><sup id="cite_ref-Ogunyemi_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ogunyemi-90"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kolawole_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kolawole-91"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup> emerged after early feminist movements were largely white and middle-class.<sup id="cite_ref-Walker_64-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walker-64"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup> Postcolonial feminists argue that colonial oppression and Western feminism marginalized postcolonial women but did not turn them passive or voiceless.<sup id="cite_ref-Weedon_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weedon-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Third-world_feminism" title="Third-world feminism" class="mw-redirect">Third-world feminism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_feminism" title="Indigenous feminism">Indigenous feminism</a> are closely related to postcolonial feminism.<sup id="cite_ref-Narayan_89-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Narayan-89"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup> These ideas also correspond with ideas in African feminism, motherism,<sup id="cite_ref-Acholonu_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Acholonu-92"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup> Stiwanism,<sup id="cite_ref-Ogundipe-Leslie_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ogundipe-Leslie-93"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup> negofeminism,<sup id="cite_ref-Nnaemeka_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nnaemeka-94"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup> femalism, <a href="/wiki/Transnational_feminism" title="Transnational feminism">transnational feminism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Africana_womanism" title="Africana womanism">Africana womanism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hudson-Weems_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hudson-Weems-95"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_constructionist_ideologies">Social constructionist ideologies</span></h3>
<p>In the late twentieth century various feminists began to argue that <a href="/wiki/Gender_roles" title="Gender roles" class="mw-redirect">gender roles</a> are <a href="/wiki/Social_construction" title="Social construction" class="mw-redirect">socially constructed</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Butler_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Butler-96"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-West.26Zimmerman_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-West.26Zimmerman-97"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup> and that it is impossible to generalize women's experiences across cultures and histories.<sup id="cite_ref-Benhabib_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Benhabib-98"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Post-structural_feminism" title="Post-structural feminism">Post-structural feminism</a> draws on the philosophies of <a href="/wiki/Post-structuralism" title="Post-structuralism">post-structuralism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Deconstruction" title="Deconstruction">deconstruction</a> in order to argue that the concept of gender is created socially and culturally through <a href="/wiki/Discourse" title="Discourse">discourse</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Postmodern_feminism" title="Postmodern feminism">Postmodern feminists</a> also emphasize the social construction of gender and the discursive nature of reality,<sup id="cite_ref-Butler_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Butler-96"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup> however as Pamela Abbot et al. note, a postmodern approach to feminism highlights "the existence of multiple truths (rather than simply men and women's standpoints)".<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_movements">Cultural movements</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Flower_power" title="Flower power">Flower Power Feminists</a> were part of the transformative <a href="/wiki/Second-wave_feminism" title="Second-wave feminism">Second Feminism Wave</a> in the 1960s. The <b>peace and love</b> symbolized opposition to the violent police raids, the military troops on college campuses, and the millions of young men being sent overseas. In regards to <b>feminism</b> this was the sexual revolution for women in the 20th century. For the first time a woman's life, sex was about <i>desire</i>, not marriage. A marriage might get to wait a few years to make sure it was about love. These were all revolutionary notions. The "me" generation was all <a href="/wiki/Flower_power" title="Flower power">flower power</a> era. <a href="/wiki/Riot_grrrl" title="Riot grrrl">Riot grrrl</a> (or riot grrl) is an underground feminist <a href="/wiki/Punk_rock" title="Punk rock">punk</a> movement that started in the 1990s and is often associated with <a href="/wiki/Third-wave_feminism" title="Third-wave feminism">third-wave feminism</a>. It was grounded in the <a href="/wiki/DIY_ethic" title="DIY ethic">DIY philosophy</a> of <a href="/wiki/Punk_ideologies#Punk_ethics" title="Punk ideologies">punk values</a>. Riot grrls took an <a href="/wiki/Anti-corporate" title="Anti-corporate" class="mw-redirect">anti-corporate</a> stance of <a href="/wiki/Self-sufficiency" title="Self-sufficiency">self-sufficiency</a> and <a href="/wiki/Individualism" title="Individualism">self-reliance</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Rowe-Finbeiner_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rowe-Finbeiner-101"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup> Riot grrrl's emphasis on universal female identity and separatism often appears more closely allied with second-wave feminism than with the third wave.<sup id="cite_ref-Rosenberg_Garofalo_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rosenberg_Garofalo-102"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup> The movement encouraged and made "adolescent girls' standpoints central," allowing them to express themselves fully.<sup id="cite_ref-Code_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Code-103"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lipstick_feminism" title="Lipstick feminism">Lipstick feminism</a> is a cultural feminist movement that attempts to respond to the backlash of second-wave radical feminism of the 1960s and 1970s by reclaiming symbols of "feminine" identity such as make-up, suggestive clothing and having a sexual allure as valid and empowering personal choices.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Feminism_and_sexuality">Feminism and sexuality</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Feminist_views_on_sexuality" title="Feminist views on sexuality">Feminist views on sexuality</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Feminist_views_on_sexuality" title="Feminist views on sexuality">Feminist views on sexuality</a> vary, and have differed by historical period and by cultural context. Feminist attitudes to female sexuality have taken a few different directions. Matters such as the <a href="/wiki/Sex_industry" title="Sex industry">sex industry</a>, sexual representation in the media, and issues regarding consent to sex under conditions of male dominance have been particularly controversial among feminists. This debate has culminated in the late 1970s and the 1980s, in what came to be known as the <a href="/wiki/Feminist_sex_wars" title="Feminist sex wars">feminist sex wars</a>, which pitted <a href="/wiki/Anti-pornography_movement" title="Anti-pornography movement" class="mw-redirect">anti-pornography feminism</a> against <a href="/wiki/Sex-positive_feminism" title="Sex-positive feminism">sex-positive feminism</a>, and parts of the feminist movement were deeply divided by these debates.<sup id="cite_ref-Duggan_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Duggan-106"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hansen_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hansen-107"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gerhard_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gerhard-108"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Leidholdt_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leidholdt-109"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vance_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vance-110"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup> Feminists have taken a variety of positions on different aspects of the <a href="/wiki/Sexual_revolution" title="Sexual revolution">sexual revolution</a> from the 1960s and 70s. Over the course of the 1970s, a large number of influential women accepted lesbian and <a href="/wiki/Bisexuality" title="Bisexuality">bisexual women</a> as part of feminism.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sex_industry">Sex industry</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Sex_industry" title="Sex industry">Sex industry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Feminist_views_on_pornography" title="Feminist views on pornography" class="mw-redirect">Feminist views on pornography</a>, <a href="/wiki/Feminist_views_on_prostitution" title="Feminist views on prostitution">Feminist views on prostitution</a> and <a href="/wiki/Feminist_sex_wars" title="Feminist sex wars">Feminist sex wars</a></div>
<p>Opinions on the sex industry are diverse. Feminists critical of the sex industry generally see it as the exploitative result of patriarchal social structures which reinforce sexual and cultural attitudes complicit in rape and <a href="/wiki/Sexual_harassment" title="Sexual harassment">sexual harassment</a>. Alternately, feminists who support at least part of the sex industry argue that it can be a medium of feminist expression and a means for women to take control of their sexuality.</p>
<p>Feminist views of pornography range from condemnation of pornography as a form of <a href="/wiki/Violence_against_women" title="Violence against women">violence against women</a>, to an embracing of some forms of pornography as a medium of feminist expression.<sup id="cite_ref-Duggan_106-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Duggan-106"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hansen_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hansen-107"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gerhard_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gerhard-108"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Leidholdt_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leidholdt-109"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vance_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vance-110"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup> Feminists' views on prostitution vary, but many of these perspectives can be loosely arranged into an overarching standpoint that is generally either critical or supportive of prostitution and <a href="/wiki/Sex_work" title="Sex work">sex work</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112"><span>[</span>112<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Affirming_female_sexual_autonomy">Affirming female sexual autonomy</span></h3>
<p>For feminists, a woman's right to control her own sexuality is a key issue. Feminists such as <a href="/wiki/Catharine_MacKinnon" title="Catharine MacKinnon">Catharine MacKinnon</a> argue that women have very little control over their own bodies, with female sexuality being largely controlled and defined by men in patriarchal societies. Feminists argue that sexual violence committed by men is often rooted in ideologies of male sexual entitlement, and that these systems grant women very few legitimate options to refuse sexual advances.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup> In many cultures, men do not believe that a woman has the right to reject a man's sexual advances or to make an autonomous decision about participating in sex. Feminists argue that all cultures are, in one way or another, dominated by ideologies that largely deny women the right to decide how to express their sexuality, because men under patriarchy feel entitled to define sex on their own terms. This entitlement can take different forms, depending on the culture. In many parts of the world, especially in <a href="/wiki/Conservative" title="Conservative" class="mw-redirect">conservative</a> and religious cultures, marriage is regarded as an institution which requires a wife to be sexually available at all times, virtually without limit; thus, forcing or coercing sex on a wife is not considered a crime or even an abusive behavior.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115"><span>[</span>115<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup> In more liberal cultures, this entitlement takes the form of a general <a href="/wiki/Sexualization" title="Sexualization">sexualization</a> of the whole culture. This is played out in the <a href="/wiki/Sexual_objectification" title="Sexual objectification">sexual objectification</a> of women, with pornography and other forms of sexual entertainment creating the fantasy that all women exist solely for men's sexual pleasure, and that women are readily available and desiring to engage in sex at any time, with any man, on a man's terms.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Feminism_and_science">Feminism and science</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote">For more details on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Feminist_epistemology" title="Feminist epistemology">Feminist epistemology</a>.</div>
<p>Sandra Harding says that the "moral and political insights of the women's movement have inspired social scientists and biologists to raise critical questions about the ways traditional researchers have explained gender, sex and relations within and between the social and natural worlds."<sup id="cite_ref-Harding_method_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harding_method-118"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup> Some feminists, such as <a href="/wiki/Ruth_Hubbard" title="Ruth Hubbard">Ruth Hubbard</a> and <a href="/wiki/Evelyn_Fox_Keller" title="Evelyn Fox Keller">Evelyn Fox Keller</a>, criticize traditional <a href="/wiki/Rhetoric_of_science" title="Rhetoric of science">scientific discourse</a> as being historically biased towards a male perspective.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup> A part of the feminist research agenda is the examination of the ways in which power inequities are created and/or reinforced in scientific and academic institutions.<sup id="cite_ref-QCommRsrch_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-QCommRsrch-120"><span>[</span>120<span>]</span></a></sup> Physicist <a href="/wiki/Lisa_Randall" title="Lisa Randall">Lisa Randall</a>, appointed to a task force at Harvard by then-president <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Summers" title="Lawrence Summers">Lawrence Summers</a> after his controversial discussion of why women may be underrepresented in science and engineering, said, "I just want to see a whole bunch more women enter the field so these issues don't have to come up anymore."<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Lynn Hankinson Nelson notes that feminist empiricists find fundamental differences between the experiences of men and women. Thus, they seek to obtain knowledge through the examination of the experiences of women, and to "uncover the consequences of omitting, misdescribing, or devaluing them" to account for a range of human experience.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup> Another part of the feminist research agenda is the uncovering of ways in which power inequities are created and/or reinforced in society and in scientific and academic institutions.<sup id="cite_ref-QCommRsrch_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-QCommRsrch-120"><span>[</span>120<span>]</span></a></sup> Furthermore, despite calls for greater attention to be paid to structures of gender inequity in the academic literature, structural analyses of gender bias rarely appear in highly cited psychological journals, especially in the commonly studied areas of psychology and personality.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123"><span>[</span>123<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>One criticism of feminist epistemology is that it allows social and political values to influence its findings.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124"><span>[</span>124<span>]</span></a></sup> Susan Haack also points out that feminist epistemology reinforces traditional stereotypes about women's thinking (as intuitive and emotional, etc.), Meera Nanda further cautions that this may in fact trap women within "traditional gender roles and help justify patriarchy".<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biology_and_gender">Biology and gender</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote boilerplate seealso">For more details on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Gender_essentialism" title="Gender essentialism" class="mw-redirect">Gender essentialism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sexual_differentiation" title="Sexual differentiation">Sexual differentiation</a>.</div>
<p>Modern feminist science challenges the biological essentialist view of <a href="/wiki/Gender" title="Gender">gender</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup> For example, <a href="/wiki/Anne_Fausto-Sterling" title="Anne Fausto-Sterling">Anne Fausto-Sterling</a>'s book, <i>Myths of Gender</i>, explores the assumptions embodied in <a href="/wiki/Scientific" title="Scientific" class="mw-redirect">scientific</a> research that support a biologically <a href="/wiki/Essentialist" title="Essentialist" class="mw-redirect">essentialist</a> view of gender.<sup id="cite_ref-Fausto-Sterling_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fausto-Sterling-128"><span>[</span>128<span>]</span></a></sup> In <i><a href="/wiki/Delusions_of_Gender" title="Delusions of Gender">Delusions of Gender</a>,</i> <a href="/wiki/Cordelia_Fine" title="Cordelia Fine">Cordelia Fine</a> disputes scientific evidence that suggests that there is an innate biological difference between men's and women's minds, asserting instead that cultural and societal beliefs are the reason for differences between individuals that are commonly perceived as sex differences.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129"><span>[</span>129<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Feminist_psychology">Feminist psychology</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Feminist_psychology" title="Feminist psychology">Feminist psychology</a></div>
<p>Feminism in psychology emerged as a critique of the dominant male outlook on psychological research where only male perspectives were studied with all male subjects. As women earned doctorates in psychology, females and their issues were introduced as legitimate topics of study. Feminist psychology emphasizes social context, lived experience, and qualitative analysis.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130"><span>[</span>130<span>]</span></a></sup> Projects such as <a href="/wiki/Psychology%27s_Feminist_Voices" title="Psychology's Feminist Voices">Psychology's Feminist Voices</a> have emerged to catalogue the influence of feminist psychologists on the discipline.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131"><span>[</span>131<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Feminist_culture">Feminist culture</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Feminism_in_culture" title="Feminism in culture">Feminism in culture</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span></h3>
<p>Gender-based inquiries into and conceptualization of architecture have also come about, leading to <a href="/wiki/Feminism_and_modern_architecture" title="Feminism and modern architecture">feminism in modern architecture</a>. Piyush Mathur coined the term "archigenderic". Claiming that "architectural planning has an inextricable link with the defining and regulation of gender roles, responsibilities, rights, and limitations", Mathur came up with that term "to explore&#160;... the meaning of 'architecture' in terms of gender" and "to explore the meaning of 'gender' in terms of architecture".<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132"><span>[</span>132<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Visual_arts">Visual arts</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Feminist_art_movement" title="Feminist art movement">Feminist art movement</a></div>
<p>Corresponding with general developments within feminism, and often including such self-organizing tactics as the consciousness-raising group, the movement began in the 1960s and flourished throughout the 1970s.<sup id="cite_ref-Gopnik_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gopnik-133"><span>[</span>133<span>]</span></a></sup> Jeremy Strick, director of the Museum of Contemporary Art in Los Angeles, described the feminist art movement as "the most influential international movement of any during the postwar period", and <a href="/wiki/Peggy_Phelan" title="Peggy Phelan">Peggy Phelan</a> says that it "brought about the most far-reaching transformations in both artmaking and art writing over the past four decades".<sup id="cite_ref-Gopnik_133-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gopnik-133"><span>[</span>133<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Judy_Chicago" title="Judy Chicago">Judy Chicago</a>, who with a team of 129 created <i><a href="/wiki/The_Dinner_Party" title="The Dinner Party">The Dinner Party</a></i>, said in 2009 to <i><a href="/wiki/ARTnews" title="ARTnews">ARTnews</a></i>, "There is still an institutional lag and an insistence on a male Eurocentric narrative. We are trying to change the future: to get girls and boys to realize that women's art is not an exception—it's a normal part of art history."<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134"><span>[</span>134<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Butler_signing.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Butler_signing.jpg/220px-Butler_signing.jpg" width="220" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Butler_signing.jpg/330px-Butler_signing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Butler_signing.jpg/440px-Butler_signing.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1380" data-file-height="1055" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Butler_signing.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Octavia_Butler" title="Octavia Butler" class="mw-redirect">Octavia Butler</a>, award-winning feminist science fiction author</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/%C3%89criture_f%C3%A9minine" title="Écriture féminine">Écriture féminine</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_American_feminist_literature" title="List of American feminist literature">List of American feminist literature</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_feminist_literature" title="List of feminist literature">List of feminist literature</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_feminist_poets" title="List of feminist poets">List of feminist poets</a></div>
<p>The feminist movement produced both feminist fiction and non-fiction, and created new interest in women's writing. It also prompted a general reevaluation of women's <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_history" title="Women's history">historical</a> and academic contributions in response to the belief that women's lives and contributions have been underrepresented as areas of scholarly interest.<sup id="cite_ref-Blain_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blain-135"><span>[</span>135<span>]</span></a></sup> Much of the early period of feminist literary scholarship was given over to the rediscovery and reclamation of texts written by women. Studies like Dale Spender's <i>Mothers of the Novel</i> (1986) and Jane Spencer's <i>The Rise of the Woman Novelist</i> (1986) were ground-breaking in their insistence that women have always been writing. Commensurate with this growth in scholarly interest, various presses began the task of reissuing long-out-of-print texts. Virago Press began to publish its large list of 19th and early-20th-century novels in 1975 and became one of the first commercial presses to join in the project of reclamation.
