In the 1980s Pandora Press, responsible for publishing Spender's study, issued a companion line of 18th-century novels written by women.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136"><span>[</span>136<span>]</span></a></sup> More recently, Broadview Press continues to issue 18th- and 19th-century novels, many hitherto out of print, and the University of Kentucky has a series of republications of early women's novels. <i><a href="/wiki/A_Vindication_of_the_Rights_of_Woman" title="A Vindication of the Rights of Woman">A Vindication of the Rights of Woman</a></i> (1792) by <a href="/wiki/Mary_Wollstonecraft" title="Mary Wollstonecraft">Mary Wollstonecraft</a>, is one of the earliest works of feminist philosophy. <i><a href="/wiki/A_Room_of_One%27s_Own" title="A Room of One's Own">A Room of One's Own</a></i> (1929) by <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Woolf" title="Virginia Woolf">Virginia Woolf</a>, is noted in its argument for both a literal and figural space for women writers within a literary tradition dominated by <a href="/wiki/Patriarchy" title="Patriarchy">patriarchy</a>.</p>
<p>The widespread interest in women's writing is related to a general reassessment and expansion of the <a href="/wiki/Literary_canon" title="Literary canon" class="mw-redirect">literary canon</a>. Interest in <a href="/wiki/Post-colonial_literature" title="Post-colonial literature" class="mw-redirect">post-colonial literatures</a>, <a href="/wiki/LGBT_literature" title="LGBT literature">gay and lesbian literature</a>, writing by people of colour, working people's writing, and the cultural productions of other historically marginalized groups has resulted in a whole scale expansion of what is considered "literature," and genres hitherto not regarded as "literary," such as children's writing, journals, letters, travel writing, and many others are now the subjects of scholarly interest.<sup id="cite_ref-Blain_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blain-135"><span>[</span>135<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137"><span>[</span>137<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138"><span>[</span>138<span>]</span></a></sup> Most <a href="/wiki/Literary_genre" title="Literary genre">genres and subgenres</a> have undergone a similar analysis, so that one now sees work on the "female gothic"<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139"><span>[</span>139<span>]</span></a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/Women_in_science_fiction" title="Women in science fiction" class="mw-redirect">women's science fiction</a>.</p>
<p>According to Elyce Rae Helford, "Science fiction and fantasy serve as important vehicles for feminist thought, particularly as bridges between theory and practice."<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140"><span>[</span>140<span>]</span></a></sup> Feminist science fiction is sometimes taught at the university level to explore the role of <a href="/wiki/Social_construct" title="Social construct" class="mw-redirect">social constructs</a> in understanding gender.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141"><span>[</span>141<span>]</span></a></sup> Notable texts of this kind are <a href="/wiki/Ursula_K._Le_Guin" title="Ursula K. Le Guin">Ursula K. Le Guin</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Left_Hand_of_Darkness" title="The Left Hand of Darkness">The Left Hand of Darkness</a></i> (1969), <a href="/wiki/Joanna_Russ" title="Joanna Russ">Joanna Russ</a>' <i><a href="/wiki/The_Female_Man" title="The Female Man">The Female Man</a></i> (1970), <a href="/wiki/Octavia_Butler" title="Octavia Butler" class="mw-redirect">Octavia Butler</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Kindred_(novel)" title="Kindred (novel)">Kindred</a></i> (1979) and <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Atwood" title="Margaret Atwood">Margaret Atwood</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Handmaid%27s_Tale" title="Handmaid's Tale" class="mw-redirect">Handmaid's Tale</a></i> (1985).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_music" title="Women's music">Women's music</a></div>
<p>Women's music (or womyn's music or wimmin's music) is the music by women, for women, and about women.<sup id="cite_ref-NoSmPvtPty_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NoSmPvtPty-142"><span>[</span>142<span>]</span></a></sup> The genre emerged as a musical expression of the <a href="/wiki/Second-wave_feminist" title="Second-wave feminist" class="mw-redirect">second-wave feminist</a> movement<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143"><span>[</span>143<span>]</span></a></sup> as well as the <a href="/wiki/Labour_(economics)" title="Labour (economics)" class="mw-redirect">labor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Civil_rights" title="Civil rights" class="mw-redirect">civil rights</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Peace_movement" title="Peace movement">peace movements</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-RadHarmonies_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RadHarmonies-144"><span>[</span>144<span>]</span></a></sup> The movement was started by lesbians such as <a href="/wiki/Cris_Williamson" title="Cris Williamson">Cris Williamson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Meg_Christian" title="Meg Christian">Meg Christian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Margie_Adam" title="Margie Adam">Margie Adam</a>, African-American women activists such as <a href="/wiki/Bernice_Johnson_Reagon" title="Bernice Johnson Reagon">Bernice Johnson Reagon</a> and her group <a href="/wiki/Sweet_Honey_in_the_Rock" title="Sweet Honey in the Rock">Sweet Honey in the Rock</a>, and peace activist <a href="/wiki/Holly_Near" title="Holly Near">Holly Near</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-RadHarmonies_144-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RadHarmonies-144"><span>[</span>144<span>]</span></a></sup> Women's music also refers to the wider industry of women's music that goes beyond the performing artists to include <a href="/wiki/Studio_musicians" title="Studio musicians" class="mw-redirect">studio musicians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Record_producer" title="Record producer">producers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sound_engineer" title="Sound engineer" class="mw-redirect">sound engineers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Technician" title="Technician">technicians</a>, cover artists, distributors, <a href="/wiki/Promoter_(entertainment)" title="Promoter (entertainment)">promoters</a>, and festival organizers who are also women.<sup id="cite_ref-NoSmPvtPty_142-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NoSmPvtPty-142"><span>[</span>142<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Feminism became a principal concern of musicologists in the 1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-mus_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mus-145"><span>[</span>145<span>]</span></a></sup> Prior to this, in the 1970s, musicologists were beginning to discover women composers and performers, and had begun to review concepts of canon, genius, genre and periodization from a feminist perspective. In other words, the question of how women musicians fit into traditional music history was now being asked.<sup id="cite_ref-mus_145-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mus-145"><span>[</span>145<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Through the 1980s and 1990s, this trend continued as musicologists like <a href="/wiki/Susan_McClary" title="Susan McClary">Susan McClary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marcia_Citron" title="Marcia Citron">Marcia Citron</a> and Ruth Solie began to consider the cultural reasons for the marginalizing of women from the received body of work. Concepts such as music as gendered discourse; professionalism; reception of women's music; examination of the sites of music production; relative wealth and education of women; popular music studies in relation to women's identity; patriarchal ideas in music analysis; and notions of gender and difference are among the themes examined during this time.<sup id="cite_ref-mus_145-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mus-145"><span>[</span>145<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cinema">Cinema</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Feminist_film_theory" title="Feminist film theory">Feminist film theory</a></div>
<div class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_cinema" title="Women's cinema">Women's cinema</a></div>
<p>Feminist cinema, advocating or illustrating feminist perspectives, arose largely with the development of <a href="/wiki/Feminist_film_theory" title="Feminist film theory">feminist film theory</a> in the late '60s and early '70s. Women who were radicalized during the 1960s by political debate and so-called sexual liberation; but the failure of radicalism to produce substantive change for women galvanized them to form consciousness-raising groups and set about analysing, from different perspectives, dominant cinema's construction of women.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146"><span>[</span>146<span>]</span></a></sup> Differences were particularly marked between <a href="/wiki/Feminist_film_theory#History" title="Feminist film theory">feminists on either side of the Atlantic</a>. 1972 saw the first feminist film festivals in the U.S. and U.K. as well as the first feminist film journal, <a href="/wiki/List_of_film_periodicals" title="List of film periodicals"><i>Women and Film</i>.</a> Trailblazers from this period included <a href="/wiki/Claire_Johnston" title="Claire Johnston">Claire Johnston</a> and <a href="/wiki/Laura_Mulvey" title="Laura Mulvey">Laura Mulvey</a>, who also organised the Women's Event at the <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh_International_Film_Festival" title="Edinburgh International Film Festival">Edinburgh Film Festival</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147"><span>[</span>147<span>]</span></a></sup> Other theorists making a powerful impact on feminist film include <a href="/wiki/Teresa_de_Lauretis" title="Teresa de Lauretis">Teresa de Lauretis</a>, Anneke Smelik and <a href="/wiki/Kaja_Silverman" title="Kaja Silverman">Kaja Silverman</a>. Approaches in philosophy and psychoanalysis fuelled Feminist Film Criticism, Feminist Independent Film and Feminist Distribution.</p>
<p>It has been argued that there are two distinct approaches to independent, theoretically inspired feminist filmmaking. 'Deconstruction' concerns itself with analysing and breaking down codes of mainstream cinema, aiming to create a different relationship between the spectator and dominant cinema. The second approach, a feminist counterculture, embodies feminine writing to investigate a specifically feminine cinematic language.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148"><span>[</span>148<span>]</span></a></sup> Some recent criticism<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149"><span>[</span>149<span>]</span></a></sup> of 'feminist film' approaches has centred around a Swedish rating system called the <a href="/wiki/Bechdel_test" title="Bechdel test">Bechdel test</a>.</p>
<p>During the 1930s-1950s heyday of the big Hollywood studios, the status of women in the industry was abysmal<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150"><span>[</span>150<span>]</span></a></sup> and, while much has improved, many would argue that there is still much to be done. From art films by <a href="/wiki/Sally_Potter" title="Sally Potter">Sally Potter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Catherine_Breillat" title="Catherine Breillat">Catherine Breillat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Claire_Denis" title="Claire Denis">Claire Denis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jane_Campion" title="Jane Campion">Jane Campion</a> to action movies by <a href="/wiki/Kathryn_Bigelow" title="Kathryn Bigelow">Kathryn Bigelow</a>, women now have a stronger voice, but are only too aware of the still lingering gender gap.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151"><span>[</span>151<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_to_political_movements">Relationship to political movements</span></h2>
<p>Feminism had complex interactions with the major political movements of the twentieth century.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Socialism">Socialism</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Left-wing_politics#Social_progressivism_and_counterculture" title="Left-wing politics">Social Progressivism and Counterculture</a></div>
<p>Since the late nineteenth century some feminists have allied with socialism, whereas others have criticized socialist ideology for being insufficiently concerned about women's rights. <a href="/wiki/August_Bebel" title="August Bebel">August Bebel</a>, an early activist of the <a href="/wiki/Social_Democratic_Party_of_Germany" title="Social Democratic Party of Germany">German Social Democratic Party</a> (SPD), published his work <i>Die Frau und der Sozialismus</i>, juxtaposing the struggle for equal rights between sexes with social equality in general. In 1907 there was an International Conference of Socialist Women in <a href="/wiki/Stuttgart" title="Stuttgart">Stuttgart</a> where suffrage was described as a tool of class struggle. <a href="/wiki/Clara_Zetkin" title="Clara Zetkin">Clara Zetkin</a> of the SPD called for women's suffrage to build a "<i>socialist order, the only one that allows for a radical solution to the women's question</i>".<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152"><span>[</span>152<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hww5_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hww5-153"><span>[</span>153<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In Britain, the women's movement was allied with the <a href="/wiki/Labour_party_(UK)" title="Labour party (UK)" class="mw-redirect">Labour party</a>. In the U.S., <a href="/wiki/Betty_Friedan" title="Betty Friedan">Betty Friedan</a> emerged from a radical background to take leadership. <a href="/wiki/Radical_Women" title="Radical Women">Radical Women</a> is the oldest socialist feminist organization in the U.S. and is still active.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154"><span>[</span>154<span>]</span></a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Civil_War" title="Spanish Civil War">Spanish Civil War</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dolores_Ib%C3%A1rruri" title="Dolores Ibárruri">Dolores Ibárruri</a> (<i>La Pasionaria</i>) led the <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_Spain_(main)" title="Communist Party of Spain (main)" class="mw-redirect">Communist Party of Spain</a>. Although she supported equal rights for women, she opposed women fighting on the front and clashed with the <a href="/wiki/Anarcha-Feminism" title="Anarcha-Feminism" class="mw-redirect">anarcha-feminist</a> <a href="/wiki/Mujeres_Libres" title="Mujeres Libres">Mujeres Libres</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ib_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ib-155"><span>[</span>155<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fascism">Fascism</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Fascism_and_ideology" title="Fascism and ideology">Fascism and ideology</a></div>
<p>Fascism has been prescribed dubious stances on feminism by its practitioners and by women's groups. Amongst other demands concerning social reform presented in the <a href="/wiki/Fascist_manifesto" title="Fascist manifesto" class="mw-redirect">Fascist manifesto</a> in 1919 was expanding the suffrage to all Italian citizens of age 18 and above, including women (accomplished only in 1946, after the defeat of fascism) and eligibility for all to stand for office from age 25. This demand was particularly championed by special Fascist women's auxiliary groups such as the <i>fasci femminilli</i> and only partly realized in 1925, under pressure from Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Benito_Mussolini" title="Benito Mussolini">Benito Mussolini</a>'s more conservative coalition partners.<sup id="cite_ref-hagg_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hagg-156"><span>[</span>156<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-passmore_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-passmore-157"><span>[</span>157<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Cyprian Blamires states that although feminists were among those who opposed the rise of <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a>, feminism has a complicated relationship with the <a href="/wiki/Nazi" title="Nazi" class="mw-redirect">Nazi</a> movement as well. While Nazis glorified traditional notions of patriarchal society and its role for women, they claimed to recognize women's equality in employment.<sup id="cite_ref-Blamires_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blamires-158"><span>[</span>158<span>]</span></a></sup> However, Hitler and Mussolini declared themselves as opposed to feminism,<sup id="cite_ref-Blamires_158-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blamires-158"><span>[</span>158<span>]</span></a></sup> and after the rise of <a href="/wiki/Nazism" title="Nazism">Nazism</a> in Germany in 1933, there was a rapid dissolution of the political rights and economic opportunities that feminists had fought for during the pre-war period and to some extent during the 1920s.<sup id="cite_ref-hww5_153-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hww5-153"><span>[</span>153<span>]</span></a></sup> Georges Duby et al. note that in practice fascist society was hierarchical and emphasized male virility, with women maintaining a largely subordinate position.<sup id="cite_ref-hww5_153-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hww5-153"><span>[</span>153<span>]</span></a></sup> Blamires also notes that Neofascism has since the 1960s been hostile towards feminism and advocates that women accept "their traditional roles".<sup id="cite_ref-Blamires_158-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blamires-158"><span>[</span>158<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_rights_movement_and_anti-racism">Civil rights movement and anti-racism</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Civil_rights_movement" title="Civil rights movement" class="mw-redirect">civil rights movement</a> has influenced and informed the feminist movement and vice versa. Many Western feminists adapted the language and theories of black equality activism and drew parallels between women's rights and the rights of non-white people.<sup id="cite_ref-Levy_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levy-159"><span>[</span>159<span>]</span></a></sup> Despite the connections between the women's and civil rights movements, some tension arose during the late 1960s and early 1970s as non-white women argued that feminism was predominantly white and middle class, and did not understand and was not concerned with race issues.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160"><span>[</span>160<span>]</span></a></sup> Similarly, some women argued that the civil rights movement had sexist elements and did not adequately address minority women's concerns.<sup id="cite_ref-Levy_159-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levy-159"><span>[</span>159<span>]</span></a></sup> These criticisms created new feminist social theories about the intersections of racism, <a href="/wiki/Classism" title="Classism" class="mw-redirect">classism</a>, and sexism, and new feminisms, such as <a href="/wiki/Black_feminism" title="Black feminism">black feminism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chicana_feminism" title="Chicana feminism">Chicana feminism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161"><span>[</span>161<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162"><span>[</span>162<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Societal_impact">Societal impact</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Feminist_effects_on_society" title="Feminist effects on society">Feminist effects on society</a></div>
<p>The feminist movement has effected change in Western society, including <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_suffrage" title="Women's suffrage">women's suffrage</a>; greater access to education; more nearly equitable pay with men; the right to initiate divorce proceedings; the right of women to make individual decisions regarding pregnancy (including access to contraceptives and abortion); and the right to own property.<sup id="cite_ref-Messer-Davidow_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Messer-Davidow-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_rights">Civil rights</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CEDAW_Participation.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/CEDAW_Participation.svg/220px-CEDAW_Participation.svg.png" width="220" height="97" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/CEDAW_Participation.svg/330px-CEDAW_Participation.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/CEDAW_Participation.svg/440px-CEDAW_Participation.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="940" data-file-height="415" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CEDAW_Participation.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Participation in the Convention on the Elimination of All Forms of Discrimination Against Women.
<table class="multicol" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td style="width:50%; text-align:left; vertical-align:top;">
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #00aa00; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Signed and ratified</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #008000; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Acceded or succeeded</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #008080; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Unrecognized state, abiding by treaty</div>
</td>
<td style="width:50%; text-align:left; vertical-align:top;">
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #eeee00; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Only signed</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ff1111; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Non-signatory</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>From the 1960s on, the campaign for <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_rights" title="Women's rights">women's rights</a><sup id="cite_ref-quarterly_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-quarterly-163"><span>[</span>163<span>]</span></a></sup> was met with mixed results<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164"><span>[</span>164<span>]</span></a></sup> in the U.S. and the U.K. Other countries of the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Community" title="European Economic Community">EEC</a> agreed to ensure that discriminatory laws would be phased out across the European Community.</p>
<p>Some feminist campaigning also helped reform attitudes to <a href="/wiki/Child_sexual_abuse" title="Child sexual abuse">child sexual abuse</a>. The view that young girls cause men to have sexual intercourse with them was replaced by that of men's responsibility for their own conduct, the men being adults.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165"><span>[</span>165<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In the U.S., the <a href="/wiki/National_Organization_for_Women" title="National Organization for Women">National Organization for Women</a> (<a href="/wiki/National_Organization_for_Women" title="National Organization for Women">NOW</a>) began in 1966 to seek women's equality, including through the <a href="/wiki/Equal_Rights_Amendment" title="Equal Rights Amendment">Equal Rights Amendment</a> (<a href="/wiki/Equal_Rights_Amendment" title="Equal Rights Amendment">ERA</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166"><span>[</span>166<span>]</span></a></sup> which did not pass, although <a href="/wiki/Equal_Rights_Amendment#State_constitutions" title="Equal Rights Amendment">some states enacted their own</a>. <a href="/wiki/Reproductive_rights" title="Reproductive rights">Reproductive rights</a> in the U.S. centered on the court decision in <a href="/wiki/Roe_v._Wade" title="Roe v. Wade"><i>Roe</i> v. <i>Wade</i></a> enunciating a woman's right to choose whether to carry a pregnancy to term. Western women gained more reliable <a href="/wiki/Birth_control" title="Birth control">birth control</a>, allowing family planning and careers. The movement started in the 1910s in the U.S. under <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Sanger" title="Margaret Sanger">Margaret Sanger</a> and elsewhere under <a href="/wiki/Marie_Stopes" title="Marie Stopes">Marie Stopes</a>. In the final three decades of the 20th century, Western women knew a new freedom through <a href="/wiki/Birth_control" title="Birth control">birth control</a>, which enabled women to plan their adult lives, often making way for both career and family.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167"><span>[</span>167<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Division_of_labor" title="Division of labor" class="mw-redirect">division of labor</a> within households was affected by the increased entry of women into workplaces in the 20th century. Sociologist <a href="/wiki/Arlie_Russell_Hochschild" title="Arlie Russell Hochschild">Arlie Russell Hochschild</a> found that, in two-career couples, men and women, on average, spend about equal amounts of time working, but women still spend more time on housework,<sup id="cite_ref-Hochschild1_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hochschild1-168"><span>[</span>168<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hochschild2_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hochschild2-169"><span>[</span>169<span>]</span></a></sup> although <a href="/wiki/Cathy_Young" title="Cathy Young">Cathy Young</a> responded by arguing that women may prevent equal participation by men in housework and parenting.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170"><span>[</span>170<span>]</span></a></sup> Judith K. Brown writes, "Women are most likely to make a substantial contribution when subsistence activities have the following characteristics: the participant is not obliged to be far from home; the tasks are relatively monotonous and do not require rapt concentration; and the work is not dangerous, can be performed in spite of interruptions, and is easily resumed once interrupted."<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171"><span>[</span>171<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In international law, the <i><a href="/wiki/Convention_on_the_Elimination_of_All_Forms_of_Discrimination_Against_Women" title="Convention on the Elimination of All Forms of Discrimination Against Women">Convention on the Elimination of All Forms of Discrimination Against Women</a></i> (CEDAW) is an international convention adopted by the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly" title="United Nations General Assembly">United Nations General Assembly</a> and described as an international <a href="/wiki/Bill_of_rights" title="Bill of rights">bill of rights</a> for women. It came into force in those nations ratifying it.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172"><span>[</span>172<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jurisprudence">Jurisprudence</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Feminist_legal_theory" title="Feminist legal theory">Feminist legal theory</a></div>
<p>Feminist jurisprudence is a branch of <a href="/wiki/Jurisprudence" title="Jurisprudence">jurisprudence</a> that examines the relationship between women and law. It addresses questions about the history of legal and social biases against women and about the enhancement of their legal rights.<sup id="cite_ref-blacks_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blacks-173"><span>[</span>173<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Feminist jurisprudence signifies a reaction to the <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_law" title="Philosophy of law">philosophical approach</a> of modern <a href="/wiki/Law#Legal_theory" title="Law">legal scholars</a>, who typically see law as a process for interpreting and perpetuating a society's universal, gender-neutral ideals. Feminist legal scholars claim that this fails to acknowledge women's values or legal interests or the harms that they may anticipate or experience.<sup id="cite_ref-minda_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-minda-174"><span>[</span>174<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote">For more details on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Gender-neutral_language_in_English" title="Gender-neutral language in English" class="mw-redirect">Gender-neutral language in English</a>.</div>
<p>Proponents of gender-neutral language argue that the use of gender-specific language often implies male superiority or reflects an unequal state of society.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175"><span>[</span>175<span>]</span></a></sup> According to <i>The Handbook of English Linguistics</i>, generic masculine pronouns and gender-specific job titles are instances "where English linguistic convention has historically treated men as prototypical of the human species."<sup id="cite_ref-handbook_linguistics_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-handbook_linguistics-176"><span>[</span>176<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Theology">Theology</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Feminist_theology" title="Feminist theology">Feminist theology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gender_of_God" title="Gender of God">Gender of God</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_080123-N-3385W-028_Cmdr._Adrienne_Simmons,_medical_provider_for_Provincial_Reconstruction_Team_Khost_and_only_woman_on_the_team,_speaks_at_the_groundbreaking_ceremony_for_a_women%27s_mosque_and_park_in_downtown_Khost_City.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/US_Navy_080123-N-3385W-028_Cmdr._Adrienne_Simmons%2C_medical_provider_for_Provincial_Reconstruction_Team_Khost_and_only_woman_on_the_team%2C_speaks_at_the_groundbreaking_ceremony_for_a_women%27s_mosque_and_park_in_downtown_Khost_City.jpg/220px-thumbnail.jpg" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/US_Navy_080123-N-3385W-028_Cmdr._Adrienne_Simmons%2C_medical_provider_for_Provincial_Reconstruction_Team_Khost_and_only_woman_on_the_team%2C_speaks_at_the_groundbreaking_ceremony_for_a_women%27s_mosque_and_park_in_downtown_Khost_City.jpg/330px-thumbnail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/US_Navy_080123-N-3385W-028_Cmdr._Adrienne_Simmons%2C_medical_provider_for_Provincial_Reconstruction_Team_Khost_and_only_woman_on_the_team%2C_speaks_at_the_groundbreaking_ceremony_for_a_women%27s_mosque_and_park_in_downtown_Khost_City.jpg/440px-thumbnail.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2504" data-file-height="1762" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_080123-N-3385W-028_Cmdr._Adrienne_Simmons,_medical_provider_for_Provincial_Reconstruction_Team_Khost_and_only_woman_on_the_team,_speaks_at_the_groundbreaking_ceremony_for_a_women%27s_mosque_and_park_in_downtown_Khost_City.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Cmdr. Adrienne Simmons speaking at the 2008 ceremony for the only women's mosque in Khost City, a symbol of progress for growing women's rights in the Pashtun belt.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Feminist theology is a movement that reconsiders the traditions, practices, scriptures, and theologies of religions from a feminist perspective. Some of the goals of feminist theology include increasing the role of women among the clergy and religious authorities, reinterpreting male-dominated imagery and language about God, determining women's place in relation to career and motherhood, and studying images of women in the religion's sacred texts.<sup id="cite_ref-Bundesen_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bundesen-177"><span>[</span>177<span>]</span></a></sup> The Christian Bible refers to women in positions of authority in <a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/bibleversefinder/?book=Judges&amp;verse=4:4&amp;src=NASB">Judges 4:4</a> and <a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/bibleversefinder/?book=2%20Kings&amp;verse=22:14&amp;src=NASB">2&#160;Kings 22:14</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Christian_feminism" title="Christian feminism">Christian feminism</a> is a branch of feminist theology which seeks to interpret and understand Christianity in light of the equality of women and men, and that this interpretation is necessary for a complete understanding of Christianity. While there is no standard set of beliefs among Christian feminists, most agree that God does not discriminate on the basis of sex, and are involved in issues such as the <a href="/wiki/Ordination_of_women" title="Ordination of women">ordination of women</a>, male dominance and the balance of parenting in Christian marriage, claims of moral deficiency and inferiority of women compared to men, and the overall treatment of women in the church.<sup id="cite_ref-Haddad_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haddad-178"><span>[</span>178<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-andeson_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-andeson-179"><span>[</span>179<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Islamic_feminism" title="Islamic feminism">Islamic feminists</a> advocate <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_rights" title="Women's rights">women's rights</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gender_equality" title="Gender equality">gender equality</a>, and social justice grounded within an Islamic framework. Advocates seek to highlight the deeply rooted teachings of equality in the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a> and encourage a questioning of the patriarchal interpretation of Islamic teaching through the Quran, <i><a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">hadith</a></i> (sayings of <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a>), and <i><a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">sharia</a></i> (law) towards the creation of a more equal and just society.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180"><span>[</span>180<span>]</span></a></sup> Although rooted in Islam, the movement's pioneers have also utilized secular and Western feminist discourses and recognize the role of Islamic feminism as part of an integrated global feminist movement.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181"><span>[</span>181<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Jewish_feminism" title="Jewish feminism">Jewish feminism</a> is a movement that seeks to improve the religious, legal, and social status of women within Judaism and to open up new opportunities for religious experience and leadership for Jewish women. The main issues for early Jewish feminists in these movements were the exclusion from the all-male prayer group or <i><a href="/wiki/Minyan" title="Minyan">minyan</a></i>, the exemption from positive time-bound <i><a href="/wiki/Mitzvah" title="Mitzvah">mitzvot</a></i>, and women's inability to function as witnesses and to initiate <a href="/wiki/Jewish_view_of_marriage#Divorce" title="Jewish view of marriage" class="mw-redirect">divorce</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Plaskow1997_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Plaskow1997-182"><span>[</span>182<span>]</span></a></sup> Many Jewish women have become leaders of feminist movements throughout their history.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183"><span>[</span>183<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Dianic_Wicca" title="Dianic Wicca">Dianic Wicca</a> is a feminist-centered <a href="/wiki/Thealogy" title="Thealogy">thealogy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184"><span>[</span>184<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Secular or <a href="/wiki/Atheist_feminism" title="Atheist feminism">atheist feminists</a> have engaged in feminist criticism of religion, arguing that many religions have oppressive rules towards women and <a href="/wiki/Misogynistic" title="Misogynistic" class="mw-redirect">misogynistic</a> themes and elements in religious texts.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185"><span>[</span>185<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186"><span>[</span>186<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187"><span>[</span>187<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Patriarchy">Patriarchy</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Patriarchy" title="Patriarchy">Patriarchy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:%22Female_Muslims-_The_tsar,_beys_and_khans_took_your_rights_away%22_%E2%80%93_Azeri,_Baku,_1921_(Mardjani).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/%22Female_Muslims-_The_tsar%2C_beys_and_khans_took_your_rights_away%22_%E2%80%93_Azeri%2C_Baku%2C_1921_%28Mardjani%29.jpg/180px-%22Female_Muslims-_The_tsar%2C_beys_and_khans_took_your_rights_away%22_%E2%80%93_Azeri%2C_Baku%2C_1921_%28Mardjani%29.jpg" width="180" height="279" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/%22Female_Muslims-_The_tsar%2C_beys_and_khans_took_your_rights_away%22_%E2%80%93_Azeri%2C_Baku%2C_1921_%28Mardjani%29.jpg/270px-%22Female_Muslims-_The_tsar%2C_beys_and_khans_took_your_rights_away%22_%E2%80%93_Azeri%2C_Baku%2C_1921_%28Mardjani%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/%22Female_Muslims-_The_tsar%2C_beys_and_khans_took_your_rights_away%22_%E2%80%93_Azeri%2C_Baku%2C_1921_%28Mardjani%29.jpg/360px-%22Female_Muslims-_The_tsar%2C_beys_and_khans_took_your_rights_away%22_%E2%80%93_Azeri%2C_Baku%2C_1921_%28Mardjani%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="386" data-file-height="599" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%22Female_Muslims-_The_tsar,_beys_and_khans_took_your_rights_away%22_%E2%80%93_Azeri,_Baku,_1921_(Mardjani).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
"Female Muslims- The tsar, beys and khans took your rights away" – Soviet poster issued in <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a>, 1921</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Patriarchy is a social system in which society is organized around male authority figures. In this system fathers have authority over women, children, and property. It implies the institutions of male rule and privilege, and is dependent on female subordination.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188"><span>[</span>188<span>]</span></a></sup> Most forms of feminism characterize patriarchy as an unjust social system that is <a href="/wiki/Oppression" title="Oppression">oppressive</a> to women. <a href="/wiki/Carole_Pateman" title="Carole Pateman">Carole Pateman</a> argues that the patriarchal distinction "between masculinity and femininity is the political difference between freedom and subjection."<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189"><span>[</span>189<span>]</span></a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Feminist_theory" title="Feminist theory">feminist theory</a> the concept of patriarchy often includes all the social mechanisms that reproduce and exert male dominance over women. Feminist theory typically characterizes patriarchy as a social construction, which can be overcome by revealing and critically analyzing its manifestations.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190"><span>[</span>190<span>]</span></a></sup> Some <a href="/wiki/Radical_feminism" title="Radical feminism">radical feminists</a> have proposed that because patriarchy is too deeply rooted in society, <a href="/wiki/Separatist_feminism" title="Separatist feminism">separatism</a> is the only viable solution.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191"><span>[</span>191<span>]</span></a></sup> Other feminists have criticized these views as being anti-men.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192"><span>[</span>192<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193"><span>[</span>193<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194"><span>[</span>194<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Men_and_masculinity">Men and masculinity</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Men_and_feminism" title="Men and feminism">Men and feminism</a></div>
<p>Feminist theory has explored the social construction of masculinity and its implications for the goal of gender equality. The social construct of masculinity is seen by feminism as problematic because it associates males with aggression and competition, and reinforces <a href="/wiki/Patriarchy" title="Patriarchy">patriarchal</a> and unequal gender relations.<sup id="cite_ref-Faludi_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Faludi-63"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195"><span>[</span>195<span>]</span></a></sup> Patriarchal cultures are criticized for "limiting forms of masculinity" available to men and thus narrowing their life choices.<sup id="cite_ref-Gardiner_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gardiner-196"><span>[</span>196<span>]</span></a></sup> Some feminists are engaged with men's issues activism, such as bringing attention to male rape and spousal battery and addressing negative social expectations for men.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197"><span>[</span>197<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198"><span>[</span>198<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199"><span>[</span>199<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Male participation in feminism is encouraged by feminists and is seen as an important strategy for achieving full societal commitment to gender equality.<sup id="cite_ref-hooks_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hooks-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200"><span>[</span>200<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201"><span>[</span>201<span>]</span></a></sup> Many male feminists and pro-feminists are active in both women's rights activism, feminist theory, and masculinity studies. However, some argue that while male engagement with feminism is necessary, it is problematic due to the ingrained social influences of patriarchy in gender relations.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202"><span>[</span>202<span>]</span></a></sup> The consensus today in feminist and masculinity theories is that both genders can and should cooperate to achieve the larger goals of feminism.<sup id="cite_ref-Gardiner_196-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gardiner-196"><span>[</span>196<span>]</span></a></sup> It has been proposed that, in large part, this can be achieved through considerations of women's <a href="/wiki/Agency_(sociology)" title="Agency (sociology)">agency</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203"><span>[</span>203<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reactions">Reactions</span></h2>
<p>Different groups of people have responded to feminism, and both men and women have been among its supporters and critics. Among American university students, for both men and women, support for feminist ideas is more common than self-identification as a feminist.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204"><span>[</span>204<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205"><span>[</span>205<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206"><span>[</span>206<span>]</span></a></sup> The US media tends to portray feminism negatively and feminists "are less often associated with day-to-day work/leisure activities of regular women."<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207"><span>[</span>207<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208"><span>[</span>208<span>]</span></a></sup> However, as recent research has demonstrated, as people are exposed to self-identified feminists and to discussions relating to various forms of feminism, their own self-identification with feminism increases.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209"><span>[</span>209<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Roy_Baumeister" title="Roy Baumeister">Roy Baumeister</a> has criticized feminists who "look only at the top of society and draw conclusions about society as a whole. Yes, there are mostly men at the top. But if you look at the bottom, really at the bottom, you'll find mostly men there, too."<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210"><span>[</span>210<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pro-feminism">Pro-feminism</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pro-feminism" title="Pro-feminism">Pro-feminism</a></div>
<p>Pro-feminism is the support of feminism without implying that the supporter is a member of the feminist movement. The term is most often used in reference to men who are actively supportive of feminism. The activities of pro-feminist men's groups include anti-violence work with boys and young men in schools, offering <a href="/wiki/Sexual_harassment" title="Sexual harassment">sexual harassment</a> workshops in workplaces, running community education campaigns, and counseling male perpetrators of violence. Pro-feminist men also may be involved in men's health, activism against pornography including anti-pornography legislation, <a href="/wiki/Men%27s_studies" title="Men's studies">men's studies</a>, and the development of <a href="/wiki/Gender_equality" title="Gender equality">gender equity</a> curricula in schools. This work is sometimes in collaboration with feminists and women's services, such as domestic violence and rape crisis centers.<sup id="cite_ref-Lingard_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lingard-211"><span>[</span>211<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kimmel-Tide_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kimmel-Tide-212"><span>[</span>212<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Critique_of_feminism_and_anti-feminism">Critique of feminism and anti-feminism</span></h3>
<p><span id="Critique_of_Feminism_and_Anti-feminism"></span></p>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Anti-feminism" title="Anti-feminism" class="mw-redirect">Anti-feminism</a></div>
<p>Anti-feminism is opposition to feminism in some or all of its forms.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213"><span>[</span>213<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In the nineteenth century, anti-feminism was mainly focused on opposition to women's suffrage. Later, opponents of women's entry into institutions of higher learning argued that education was too great a physical burden on women. Other anti-feminists opposed women's entry into the labor force, or their right to join unions, to sit on juries, or to obtain birth control and control of their sexuality.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214"><span>[</span>214<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Some people have opposed feminism on the grounds that they believe it is contrary to traditional values or religious beliefs. These anti-feminists argue, for example, that social acceptance of divorce and non-married women is wrong and harmful, and that men and women are fundamentally different and thus their different traditional roles in society should be maintained.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215"><span>[</span>215<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216"><span>[</span>216<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217"><span>[</span>217<span>]</span></a></sup> Other anti-feminists oppose women's entry into the workforce, political office, and the voting process, as well as the lessening of male authority in families.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218"><span>[</span>218<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219"><span>[</span>219<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Writers such as <a href="/wiki/Camille_Paglia" title="Camille Paglia">Camille Paglia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christina_Hoff_Sommers" title="Christina Hoff Sommers">Christina Hoff Sommers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jean_Bethke_Elshtain" title="Jean Bethke Elshtain">Jean Bethke Elshtain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Fox-Genovese" title="Elizabeth Fox-Genovese">Elizabeth Fox-Genovese</a>, Lisa Lucile Owens<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220"><span>[</span>220<span>]</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Daphne_Patai" title="Daphne Patai">Daphne Patai</a> oppose some forms of feminism, though they identify as feminists. They argue, for example, that feminism often promotes <a href="/wiki/Misandry" title="Misandry">misandry</a> and the elevation of women's interests above men's, and criticize radical feminist positions as harmful to both men and women.<sup id="cite_ref-Hoff_Sommers_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hoff_Sommers-221"><span>[</span>221<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Disputed statement" class="mw-redirect"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (October 2015)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata">– <a href="/wiki/Talk:Feminism#Who_stole_feminism.3F_quote" title="Talk:Feminism">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup> <a href="/wiki/Daphne_Patai" title="Daphne Patai">Daphne Patai</a> and Noretta Koertge argue that the term "anti-feminist" is used to silence academic debate about feminism.<sup id="cite_ref-Patai_Koertge_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Patai_Koertge-222"><span>[</span>222<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223"><span>[</span>223<span>]</span></a></sup> Lisa Lucile Owens argues that certain rights extended exclusively to women are patriarchal because they relieve women from exercising a crucial aspect of their moral agency.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224"><span>[</span>224<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<li id="cite_note-215"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-215">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite id="CITEREFLukas2006" class="citation"><a href="/wiki/Carrie_Lukas" title="Carrie Lukas">Lukas, Carrie</a> (2006), "Marriage: happier ever after", in <a href="/wiki/Carrie_Lukas" title="Carrie Lukas">Lukas, Carrie</a>, <i>The politically incorrect guide to women, sex, and feminism</i>, Washington, DC Lanham, Maryland: Regency Publishing, p.&#160;75, <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781596980037" title="Special:BookSources/9781596980037">9781596980037</a>, <q>Feminists' assault on marriage also has played a role in devaluing marriage. <li id="cite_note-216"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-216">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite id="CITEREFKassian2005" class="citation">Kassian, Mary (2005), "Introduction: the tsunami of feminism", in Kassian, Mary, <i>The feminist <del>mystique</del> mistake: the radical impact of feminism on church and culture</i> (2nd ed.), Wheaton, Illinois: <a href="/wiki/Good_News_Publishers" title="Good News Publishers">Crossway Books</a>, p.&#160;10, <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781581345704" title="Special:BookSources/9781581345704">9781581345704</a>, <q>The feminist assault on traditional gender roles and families began in earnest in the 1960s and increasingly turned radical in the 1970s.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFeminism&amp;rft.atitle=Introduction%3A+the+tsunami+of+feminism&amp;rft.aufirst=Mary&amp;rft.aulast=Kassian&amp;rft.btitle=The+feminist+%3Cdel%3Emystique%3C%2Fdel%3E+mistake%3A+the+radical+impact+of+feminism+on+church+and+culture&amp;rft.date=2005&amp;rft.edition=2nd&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.isbn=9781581345704&amp;rft.pages=10&amp;rft.place=Wheaton%2C+Illinois&amp;rft.pub=Crossway+Books&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-217"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-217">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book"><a href="/wiki/Phyllis_Schlafly" title="Phyllis Schlafly">Schlafly, Phyllis</a> (1977). "Understanding the difference". In <a href="/wiki/Phyllis_Schlafly" title="Phyllis Schlafly">Schlafly, Phyllis</a>. <i>The power of the positive woman</i>. New Rochelle, New York: Arlington House. p.&#160;12. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780870003738" title="Special:BookSources/9780870003738">9780870003738</a>. <q>The second dogma of the women's liberationists is that, of all the injustices perpetuated upon women through the centuries, the most oppressive is the cruel fact that women have babies and men do not. Within the confines of the women's liberationist ideology, therefore, the abolition of this overriding inequality of women becomes the primary goal. This goal must be achieved at any at all costs - to the woman herself, to the baby, to the family, and to society. Women must be made equal to men in their ability <i>not</i> to become pregnant and <i>not</i> to be expected to care for babies they may bring into the world.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFeminism&amp;rft.atitle=Understanding+the+difference&amp;rft.aufirst=Phyllis&amp;rft.aulast=Schlafly&amp;rft.btitle=The+power+of+the+positive+woman&amp;rft.date=1977&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.isbn=9780870003738&amp;rft.pages=12&amp;rft.place=New+Rochelle%2C+New+York&amp;rft.pub=Arlington+House&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-218"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-218">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation news"><a href="/wiki/Paul_Gottfried" title="Paul Gottfried">Gottfried, Paul</a> (21 April 2001). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.lewrockwell.com/2001/04/paul-gottfried/the-trouble-with-feminism/">"The trouble with feminism"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/LewRockwell.com" title="LewRockwell.com">LewRockwell.com</a> (web magazine)</i> (<a href="/wiki/Lew_Rockwell" title="Lew Rockwell">Lew Rockwell</a>)<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">30 September</span> 2006</span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFeminism&amp;rft.atitle=The+trouble+with+feminism&amp;rft.aufirst=Paul&amp;rft.aulast=Gottfried&amp;rft.date=21+April+2001&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.lewrockwell.com%2F2001%2F04%2Fpaul-gottfried%2Fthe-trouble-with-feminism%2F&amp;rft.jtitle=LewRockwell.com+%28web+magazine%29&amp;rft.pub=Lew+Rockwell&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-219"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-219">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite id="CITEREFal-Qaradawi2008" class="citation"><a href="/wiki/Yusuf_al-Qaradawi" title="Yusuf al-Qaradawi">al-Qaradawi, Yusuf</a> (2008), "Women and family in Islamist discourses: "When Islam prohibits something, it closes all the avenues of approach to it<span style="padding-right:0.2em;">"</span>", in <a href="/wiki/John_Calvert_(scholar)" title="John Calvert (scholar)">Calvert, John</a>, <i>Islamism: a documentary and reference guide</i>, Westport, Conn: Greenwood Press, p.&#160;62, <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780313338564" title="Special:BookSources/9780313338564">9780313338564</a>, <q>Islamists are aggrieved at the support of ostensibly Muslim governments for the "alleged" legal emancipation of women, including granting women the right to vote and hold public office, in addition to limited rights to initiate divorce. Although many Muslim women take pride in the fact that they now perform jobs and enter professions once reserved for men, for most Islamists female employment and legal emancipation are dangerous trends that lead to the dissolution of traditional gender roles associated with the extended family.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFeminism&amp;rft.atitle=Women+and+family+in+Islamist+discourses%3A+%22When+Islam+prohibits+something%2C+it+closes+all+the+avenues+of+approach+to+it%22&amp;rft.aufirst=Yusuf&amp;rft.aulast=al-Qaradawi&amp;rft.btitle=Islamism%3A+a+documentary+and+reference+guide&amp;rft.date=2008&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.isbn=9780313338564&amp;rft.pages=62&amp;rft.place=Westport%2C+Conn&amp;rft.pub=Greenwood+Press&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>

<li id="cite_note-Patai_Koertge-222"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Patai_Koertge_222-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite id="CITEREFPatai2003" class="citation"><a href="/wiki/Daphne_Patai" title="Daphne Patai">Patai, Daphne</a> (2003), "Policing the academy: "<i>Anti-feminist intellectual harassment</i>"", in <a href="/wiki/Daphne_Patai" title="Daphne Patai">Patai, Daphne</a>; Koertge, Noretta, <i>Professing feminism: education and indoctrination in women's studies</i>, Lanham, Maryland: Lexington Books, pp.&#160;278–279, <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780739104552" title="Special:BookSources/9780739104552">9780739104552</a>, <q>...the book [<i>Antifeminism in the Academy</i> by Clark, Vévé <i>et al</i>] attempts to extend an already dubious concept - hostile environment harassment - to encompass a whole new range of thought and behavior.
